Shen Bao Amateur Weekly

The Current Situation of China's Silk Industry

— Liu Ming

China is the oldest silk-producing country in the world. Logically, China's silk industry should have made special progress compared to other countries. However, upon close examination of the history of China's silk industry in recent years, the facts are quite different. Not only has the amount of silk exported gradually decreased, but the production of raw silk has also greatly declined. Exports have decreased from 189,000 dan (worth 147 million customs taels) in 1929 to 78,000 dan (worth only 32 million customs taels) in 1932. Regarding the production of raw silk, China has never had accurate statistics. However, judging from the recent decline of China's silk reeling industry and the sharp decline in exports over the years, we can know its approximate situation.

This decrease in exports and decline in production is partly due to the global economic panic, but the main reason lies in the backwardness of China's silk production technology. In name, China is the oldest silk-producing country, but in reality, the production methods currently used are completely following in the footsteps of other countries.

However, even in this problem of "following," there is still a sense of lagging behind.

The development of China's silk industry is primarily in Jiangsu and Zhejiang. Next are Sichuan, Shandong, Anhui, and Hubei. The silk produced in Jiangsu and Zhejiang is mainly White Filature silk and Tussah silk, while Sichuan and Shandong mainly produce Yellow silk. High-quality White Filature silk was once priced at over 1,000 taels (per dan) in early 1932, but in 1933, it consistently hovered around 300 to 700 yuan (with only a price of 800 yuan in July). However, in terms of production costs, the spring cocoon costs 650 yuan, the summer cocoon costs 950 yuan, and the autumn cocoon costs 800 yuan. Under these circumstances, those engaged in the silk industry can only lose money.

Regarding the selling prices and costs of Tussah silk, Sichuan Yellow silk, and Shandong Yellow silk, the same can be inferred.

This situation of drastic price drops is partly due to the stagnation of foreign sales and partly due to excessive silk stocks.

Secondly, in terms of cocoon prices, the situation is also the same. Before 1929, each dan of native cocoons was worth 7080 yuan, but recently, even improved varieties are only worth 4050 yuan, while native varieties are only worth 2030 yuan.

Under these circumstances, although the government issued silk industry bonds and established the Jiangsu-Zhejiang Silk and Cocoon Registration and Promotion Committee last year (after registration, the government subsidized each dan of silk and cocoon exports with 140 yuan of public bonds, and the export tax was waived), due to various reasons, it only remedied a portion of the old silk and cocoons. After the two committees ended in May of last year, silk and cocoon prices gradually plummeted again. Subsequently, although the Jiangsu-Zhejiang Silk Industry Joint Control Committee in February of last year and the Silk Improvement Committee led by the National Economic Committee in February of this year have successively emerged, there have been no concrete achievements demonstrated until recently.

Looking at the overall situation of China's silk industry in recent years, we can say that it is in a continuous downward spiral. To reverse this downward trend, we believe that: first, we must actively start by improving silkworm varieties and improving production technology. Only with excellent technology and superior quality can we compete with foreign goods in the international silk market. Second, in terms of passive measures, we must not only expand overseas markets, but also seek to develop domestic sales on a large scale. Only with a developed domestic silk weaving industry can we consume a large amount of raw silk; only with a developed silk reeling industry can we consume a large amount of silkworm cocoons.

Lifestyle

A Type of Schoolwork: Knowledge of Military Training

— Qing

If you were to go to the front of the Japanese barracks in Hongkou these past few days, you would probably always see dozens of Japanese soldiers wearing chest protectors, helmets, and holding round-tipped rifles, practicing [bayonet drills] - a type of killing technique - under the blazing sun. Are they occupying us, or are we occupying them?

This year, the Ministry of Education issued a circular to all secondary and higher schools across the country, requiring student soldiers to undergo three weeks of extremely rigorous military training during this summer vacation. Although the timing seems to have already been missed, it is still a [belated remedy]: this is what is meant by [ten years of training]. My diary has therefore been filled with countless valuable materials. Now I will make this material public.

Training began on July 1st and ended on the 21st. The barracks were borrowed from our school. Our life was completely militarized, with [obedience] as the primary duty: unlike the previous military training, which only involved wearing uniforms for drills, the two are not comparable. We were urged to get up by the reveille at four o'clock in the morning, and had to complete dressing, making the bed, and washing up within twenty minutes: then we went to the drill ground for roll call. From 4:30 to 5:00, we did flexibility exercises for half an hour. From 5:00 onwards, it was technical training, with special emphasis on squad drills. Breakfast was at 6:30. From 7:30 to
